<p>Hurricane Betsy slammed into <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> on the evening of September 9, 1965. 110 mph (180 km/h) winds and power failures were reported in New Orleans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[89]</a></sup> The eye of the storm passed to the southwest of New Orleans on a northwesterly track. The northern and western eyewalls covered Southeast Louisiana and the New Orleans area from about 8 pm until 4 am the next morning. In <a href="/wiki/Thibodaux,_Louisiana" title="Thibodaux, Louisiana">Thibodaux</a> winds of 130 mph (210 km/h) to 140 mph (230 km/h) were reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[90]</a></sup> The Baton Rouge weather bureau operated under auxiliary power, without telephone communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[91]</a></sup> Around 1 am, the worst of the wind and rain was over.</p>
<p>Betsy also drove a <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surge</a> into <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pontchartrain" title="Lake Pontchartrain">Lake Pontchartrain</a>, just north of New Orleans, and the Mississippi River Gulf Outlet, a deep-water shipping channel to the east and south. Levees for the Mississippi River Gulf Outlet along Florida Avenue in the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Ninth_Ward" title="Lower Ninth Ward">Lower Ninth Ward</a> and on both sides of the Industrial Canal failed. The flood water reached the <a href="/wiki/Eaves" title="Eaves">eaves</a> of houses in some places and over some one story roofs in the Lower Ninth Ward. Some residents drowned in their attics trying to escape the rising waters.</p>
<p>These levee breaches flooded parts of <a href="/wiki/Gentilly,_New_Orleans" title="Gentilly, New Orleans">Gentilly</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ninth_Ward_of_New_Orleans#Upper_9th_Ward" title="Ninth Ward of New Orleans">Upper Ninth Ward</a>, and the Lower Ninth Ward of New Orleans as well as <a href="/wiki/Arabi,_Louisiana" title="Arabi, Louisiana">Arabi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chalmette,_Louisiana" title="Chalmette, Louisiana">Chalmette</a> in neighboring St. Bernard Parish. President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_Johnson" title="Lyndon Johnson">Lyndon Johnson</a> visited the city, promising New Orleans Mayor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vic_Schiro" title="Vic Schiro">Vic Schiro</a> federal aid.</p>
<p>It was ten days or more before the water level in New Orleans went down enough for people to return to their homes. It took even longer than that to restore their flooded houses to a livable condition. Those who did not have family or friends with dry homes had to sleep in the shelters at night and forage for supplies during the day, while waiting for the federal government to provide emergency relief in the form of trailers. In all, 164,000 homes were flooded at the second landfall.</p>
<p>Evidence suggests that cheap construction and poor maintenance of the structures led to the failure of the levees. However, popular rumor persists that they were intentionally breached, possibly as a means of salvaging the more prosperous <a href="/wiki/French_Quarter" title="French Quarter">French Quarter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[92]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A more detailed citation is required. (August 2011)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This is, however, unlikely; even though the French Quarter is one of the geographically highest neighborhoods in the city, during the first eighty years of the 20th century, the French Quarter was, in fact, an unfashionable neighborhood, populated mostly by lower income people, who were not priced out of the market until well into the 1980s.</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<p>Many of the barges that had been traveling on the Mississippi River were engulfed by the hurricane. One of the barges, <i>MTC-602</i>, contained 602 tons of deadly chlorine gas contained in cylinders. Chlorine gas, which was used frequently as a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_weapons_in_World_War_I" title="Chemical weapons in World War I">chemical weapon in World War I</a>, is a powerful irritant that can inflict damage to the eyes, nose, throat and lungs, and (at high concentrations and prolonged exposure) cause death by asphyxiation. It was estimated that the amount of chlorine loaded on the barge was enough to kill 40,000 people. The barge had sunk near <a href="/wiki/Baton_Rouge,_Louisiana" title="Baton Rouge, Louisiana">Baton Rouge</a>, where an estimated 300,000 people lived. The residents in the harbor area were evacuated until the barge was recovered. President Lyndon B. Johnson ordered the Navy and Army Engineers to find and raise the barge. While it took months to locate and make the appropriate plans for raising the barge, the actual process of raising it took around two hours. The barge was reportedly recovered, without any problems, on November 12, 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mississippi">Mississippi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Betsy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mississippi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The storm produced rainfall, high tides, and strong winds in Mississippi. Near the border with Alabama, tides of 7 feet (2.1 m) were reported, while ranging as high as 15 feet (4.6 m) near the state line with Louisiana. Wind speeds also varied greatly throughout the state. In <a href="/wiki/Pascagoula,_Mississippi" title="Pascagoula, Mississippi">Pascagoula</a>, winds between 40 and 65 mph (64 and 105 km/h) were recorded. By contrast, winds were in excess of 100 mph (160 km/h) in <a href="/wiki/Bay_St._Louis,_Mississippi" title="Bay St. Louis, Mississippi">Bay St. Louis</a>. Despite the winds, much of the property damage in the state was caused by tides along the Gulf Coast. Strong winds and heavy rainfall caused significant crop damage in <a href="/wiki/Harrison_County,_Mississippi" title="Harrison County, Mississippi">Harrison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hancock_County,_Mississippi" title="Hancock County, Mississippi">Hancock County, Mississippi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Mississippi" title="Jackson County, Mississippi">Jackson County, Mississippi</a>. Throughout the state, 25,000 people lost electricity and more than 22,641 disruptions to telephone service occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata-100">[95]</a></sup> Overall, damage in the state of Mississippi totaled to $80 million (1965 USD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-101">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alabama">Alabama</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Betsy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Alabama">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Though Betsy remained well south and east of <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a> throughout its existence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HURDAT_5-20"><a href="#cite_note-HURDAT-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPCRain_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-WPCRain-29">[25]</a></sup> its outer rainbands and strong storm surge caused damage in some areas of the state, particularly in the southern portions of the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCR-2_25-9"><a href="#cite_note-TCR-2-25">[21]</a></sup> At the coast, the storm tide caused by Betsy were the highest since <a href="/wiki/1916_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="1916 Atlantic hurricane season">1916</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlabamaTCR1_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlabamaTCR1-102">[97]</a></sup> High tides peaking at 4.7 ft (56 in) in <a href="/wiki/Mobile,_Alabama" title="Mobile, Alabama">Mobile</a> destroyed and damage some private piers and waterfront buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StormData_80-7"><a href="#cite_note-StormData-80">[75]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Bay" title="Mobile Bay">Mobile Bay Causeway</a> and adjacent infrastructure was flooded by the storm surge; as a result the causeway was closed between September 9–10.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlabamaTCR1_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-AlabamaTCR1-102">[97]</a></sup> Strong winds were also reported in southern Alabama. Gusts of 80 mph (130 km/h) were reported on <a href="/wiki/Dauphin_Island,_Alabama" title="Dauphin Island, Alabama">Dauphin Island</a> just off the Alabama coast; these would be the strongest winds or gusts reported statewide. Similarly, gusts of 75 mph (121 km/h) were estimated at <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Port,_Alabama" title="Alabama Port, Alabama">Alabama Port</a>. In the former, minor damage was reported to residences and other buildings, and numerous homes sustained minor roof and carport damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlabamaTCR2_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlabamaTCR2-103">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mobile_County,_Alabama" title="Mobile County, Alabama">Mobile County</a> reported similar damage. Extensive damage to utility lines in those same regions also occurred. The strong winds also resulted in the tearing of 20–25 percent of the state pecan crop from their trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StormData_80-8"><a href="#cite_note-StormData-80">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlabamaTCR2_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-AlabamaTCR2-103">[98]</a></sup> Damage to other crops was negligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlabamaTCR2_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-AlabamaTCR2-103">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although rainfall occurred throughout Alabama, precipitation was more numerous in the northern part of the state after Betsy passed the region to the north as a weakening tropical cyclone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPCRain_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-WPCRain-29">[25]</a></sup> Rainfall peaked at 3.39 in (86 mm) in <a href="/wiki/Guntersville,_Alabama" title="Guntersville, Alabama">Guntersville</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GCRain_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-GCRain-104">[99]</a></sup> Betsy's outer rainbands also produced two tornadoes in the state. The first occurred in a remote area near <a href="/wiki/Theodore,_Alabama" title="Theodore, Alabama">Theodore</a> and as such did not cause any damage. However, the second tornado, which touched down near <a href="/wiki/Cullman,_Alabama" title="Cullman, Alabama">Cullman</a> late on September 11, destroyed several acres of corn and uprooted over 300 fruit trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlabamaTCR1_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-AlabamaTCR1-102">[97]</a></sup> The tornado, described as one of "narrow" length, also slightly damaged some buildings and uprooted a number of other trees. Though no exact damage total could be calculated, the tornado caused anywhere between $5,500–$55,000 in damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StormData_80-9"><a href="#cite_note-StormData-80">[75]</a></sup> Statewide, Hurricane Betsy caused $500,000 in damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR-Season_2-30"><a href="#cite_note-MWR-Season-2">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elsewhere">Elsewhere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Betsy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Elsewhere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Betsy_Arkansas_rainfall.gif"><img alt="Black and white contoured map of precipitation in a state. Cities and county borders are marked." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="749" data-file-width="864" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Hurricane_Betsy_Arkansas_rainfall.gif/220px-Hurricane_Betsy_Arkansas_rainfall.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Hurricane_Betsy_Arkansas_rainfall.gif/330px-Hurricane_Betsy_Arkansas_rainfall.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Hurricane_Betsy_Arkansas_rainfall.gif/440px-Hurricane_Betsy_Arkansas_rainfall.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Betsy_Arkansas_rainfall.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rainfall totals in Arkansas</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In its early formative stages, Betsy forced the shortening of NASA's <a href="/wiki/Gemini_5" title="Gemini 5">Gemini 5</a> mission by one orbit due to the tropical storm's forecasted track over the initial target <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Splashdown_(spacecraft_landing)" title="Splashdown (spacecraft landing)">splashdown</a> zone near Grand Turk Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeminiVTrip_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-GeminiVTrip-105">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TSBetsyGains_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-TSBetsyGains-106">[101]</a></sup> As a result, the spacecraft, which had been orbiting the Earth since August 1965, had its target splashdown zone shifted northward to an area of the Atlantic Ocean well east of Jacksonville, Florida, away from the storm's projected path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeminiVTrip_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-GeminiVTrip-105">[100]</a></sup> In Martinique, the precursor tropical depression caused marginal rainfall and light gusts, and no damage was reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TSBetsyGains_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-TSBetsyGains-106">[101]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Sint_Maarten" title="Sint Maarten">Sint Maarten</a>, winds and their associated gusts peaked at 35 mph (56 km/h) for several hours on August 28.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SanJuanBetsyReport_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-SanJuanBetsyReport-107">[102]</a></sup> Throughout the rest of the Windward Islands, gusts peaked at 40 mph (65 km/h),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR-Season_2-31"><a href="#cite_note-MWR-Season-2">[1]</a></sup> though the resultant wind damage was marginal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BetsyNowAHurricane_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-BetsyNowAHurricane-108">[103]</a></sup> As the hurricane was passing near the southeastern Bahamas, high swells were reported along the northern coast of <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>, though no damage ensued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BetsySkirt_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-BetsySkirt-67">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the more significant effects of Hurricane Betsy in the United States were limited to coastal regions, areas further inland received rainfall and strong winds from the weakening tropical cyclone and its remnants, with precipitation extending inland as far northeast as <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPCRain_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-WPCRain-29">[25]</a></sup> Far offset from where Betsy made landfall, effects in <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> were minimal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR-Season_2-32"><a href="#cite_note-MWR-Season-2">[1]</a></sup> despite the large scale evacuations in Sabine Pass prior to landfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TexasTCR_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-TexasTCR-109">[104]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Port_Arthur,_Texas" title="Port Arthur, Texas">Port Arthur</a> a station received just 0.02 in (0.51 mm) of rain, coupled with storm tides 2.4 ft (0.73 m) above mean sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR-Season_2-33"><a href="#cite_note-MWR-Season-2">[1]</a></sup> From September 10–11, Betsy passed through <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, which experienced the worst effects in the United States outside of states adjacent to the Gulf of Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArkansasTCR1_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArkansasTCR1-110">[105]</a></sup> The highest reported rainfall total from the hurricane in the state was in <a href="/wiki/Wynne,_Arkansas" title="Wynne, Arkansas">Wynne</a>, where 8.02 in (204 mm) of rain was recorded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GCRain_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-GCRain-104">[99]</a></sup> Surrounding regions in northeastern Arkansas reported at least 6 in (150 mm) of rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS-LZK_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWS-LZK-111">[106]</a></sup> The heavy precipitation and resulting flooding there damaged cotton and rice crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR-Season_2-34"><a href="#cite_note-MWR-Season-2">[1]</a></sup> Much of the rice crop in the eastern and southern portions of the state were flattened by the rains, and thus were highly susceptible to future rainfalls. A third of cotton, much of which defoliated, was lost to the rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArkansasTCR2_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArkansasTCR2-112">[107]</a></sup> However, these losses were offset by the increased soybean yield resulting from the same rainfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR-Season_2-35"><a href="#cite_note-MWR-Season-2">[1]</a></sup> A number of pecan tree limbs were torn down, though relative to the overall pecan production for the state these losses were negligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArkansasTCR2_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-ArkansasTCR2-112">[107]</a></sup> The rains also caused both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_River_(Arkansas)" title="Black River (Arkansas)">Black</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_River_(Arkansas)" title="White River (Arkansas)">White Rivers</a> in the eastern part of the state to rise from 3 ft (0.91 m) to 7 ft (2.1 m); however, they did not exceed <a href="/wiki/Flood_stage" title="Flood stage">flood stage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArkansasTCR2_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-ArkansasTCR2-112">[107]</a></sup> Betsy's remnants were estimated to have brought winds of 50–70 mph (80–113 km/h) throughout the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS-LZK_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-NWS-LZK-111">[106]</a></sup> Most of the stronger winds were in the northern quadrant of the weakening tropical cyclone as it progressed through Arkansas. However, the highest measured wind gusts were only clocked at 45 mph (72 km/h) in stations at <a href="/wiki/Pine_Bluff,_Arkansas" title="Pine Bluff, Arkansas">Pine Bluff</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walnut_Ridge,_Arkansas" title="Walnut Ridge, Arkansas">Walnut</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArkansasTCR1_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-ArkansasTCR1-110">[105]</a></sup> The strong winds tore down power lines, leaving hundreds of electricity customers without power for several days. However, regional electrical crews were able to restore most power by the night of September 11.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArkansasTCR2_112-3"><a href="#cite_note-ArkansasTCR2-112">[107]</a></sup> Four people were killed by the weakening hurricane statewide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArkansasTCR2_112-4"><a href="#cite_note-ArkansasTCR2-112">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, the remnants of Hurricane Betsy brought heavy rainfall to the extreme southern portions of the state over a period of three days, ranging anywhere from 3–6 in (76–152 mm);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StormData_80-10"><a href="#cite_note-StormData-80">[75]</a></sup> the highest recorded total was 6.25 in (159 mm) in <a href="/wiki/Cairo,_Illinois" title="Cairo, Illinois">Cairo, Illinois</a>. The same station recorded 6.25 in (159 mm) of rain in a 24-hour period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IllinoisTCR_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-IllinoisTCR-113">[108]</a></sup> The resulting damage, if any, was minimal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StormData_80-11"><a href="#cite_note-StormData-80">[75]</a></sup> though minor damage occurred to cotton and soy crops in the Cario area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KentuckyTCR3_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-KentuckyTCR3-114">[109]</a></sup> Hail and strong winds in <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Bluegrass_region" title="Bluegrass region">Bluegrass region</a> damaged <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a> fields and caused damage to 35 mobile homes and a number of other roofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StormData_80-12"><a href="#cite_note-StormData-80">[75]</a></sup> Though no deaths were directly associated with Betsy's effects, a car lost control during a rainstorm in <a href="/wiki/Gallatin_County,_Kentucky" title="Gallatin County, Kentucky">Gallatin County</a> and subsequently crashed; the two occupants later went missing and were presumed dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KentuckyTCR2_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-KentuckyTCR2-115">[110]</a></sup> Strong wind in <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_County,_Kentucky" title="Montgomery County, Kentucky">Montgomery County</a> downed trees. In western <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, moderate to heavy rains in conjunction with gusts as strong as 40 mph (65 km/h) were reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StormData_80-13"><a href="#cite_note-StormData-80">[75]</a></sup> Precipitation peaked in the state at 6.01 in (153 mm) in <a href="/wiki/Ripley,_Tennessee" title="Ripley, Tennessee">Ripley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SERain_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-SERain-116">[111]</a></sup> Though much of the rainfall was beneficial to the region's agricultural sector, localized flooding was also reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StormData_80-14"><a href="#cite_note-StormData-80">[75]</a></sup> Winds estimated between 25–35 mph (40–56 km/h) blew down some cotton and corn crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TNTCR_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-TNTCR-117">[112]</a></sup> Other opened cotton bolls were damaged, while soybeans were blown down, making mechanical harvesting difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TNTCR2_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-TNTCR2-118">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Further north and east, the rains Betsy produced were mostly beneficial as the storm had substantially weakened by the time it had approached these regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR-Season_2-36"><a href="#cite_note-MWR-Season-2">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HURDAT_5-21"><a href="#cite_note-HURDAT-5">[4]</a></sup> The outer fringes of Betsy caused moderate rainfall in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southeast_United_States" title="Southeast United States">southeastern states</a> of <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, where state precipitation totals peaked at 3.64 in (92 mm) in <a href="/wiki/Randleman,_North_Carolina" title="Randleman, North Carolina">Randleman</a>, 3.48 in (88 mm) in <a href="/wiki/Ailey,_Georgia" title="Ailey, Georgia">Ailey</a>, and 2.21 in (56 mm) in <a href="/wiki/Laurens,_South_Carolina" title="Laurens, South Carolina">Laurens</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SERain_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-SERain-116">[111]</a></sup> Waves cresting as high as 7 ft (2.1 m) off of South Carolina caused minor beach damage during Betsy's initial approach of the <a href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_States" title="South Atlantic States">South Atlantic States</a> on September 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCBetsy_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCBetsy-119">[114]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>, the rains helped to saturate soils used for growing crops, benefiting crop production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WVTCR_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-WVTCR-120">[115]</a></sup> From September 11–13, Betsy's rains were felt throughout <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>. Rainfall peaked at 1.5 in (38 mm) in the central and northeastern regions of the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PennTCR_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-PennTCR-121">[116]</a></sup> Further south, in <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>, the rains were also beneficial to arable land. Precipitation in the former peaked at 2.55 in (65 mm) in <a href="/wiki/Bittinger,_Maryland" title="Bittinger, Maryland">Bittinger</a>, while precipitation in the latter peaked at 1.83 in (46 mm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarylandTCR_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-MarylandTCR-122">[117]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Betsy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_preparedness_for_New_Orleans" title="Hurricane preparedness for New Orleans">Hurricane preparedness for New Orleans</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="U.S. Army Corps of Engineers">U.S. Army Corps of Engineers</a>' Hurricane Protection Program came into existence as a result of Betsy. The Corps built new levees for New Orleans that were both taller and made of stronger material, designed specifically to resist a fast-moving Category 3 hurricane like Betsy. The resulting levee improvements failed when <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>, a large, slow-moving, intense hurricane made landfall near <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> on August 29, 2005.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement">Retirement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Betsy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Retirement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricane_names" title="List of retired Atlantic hurricane names">List of retired Atlantic hurricane names</a></div>
<p>Due to the storm's extent and severity of impacts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retried_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Retried-123">[118]</a></sup> the name <i>Betsy</i>, which had also been used in <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Betsy_(1956)" title="Hurricane Betsy (1956)">1956</a> and <a href="/wiki/1961_Atlantic_hurricane_season#Hurricane_Betsy" title="1961 Atlantic hurricane season">1961</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR1956_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-MWR1956-124">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR1961_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-MWR1961-125">[120]</a></sup> was <a href="/wiki/List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricane_names" title="List of retired Atlantic hurricane names">retired</a> from the set of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tropical_cyclone_names" title="List of tropical cyclone names">rotating lists</a> used to name tropical cyclones in the Atlantic upon its third usage. This made the Betsy the only retired tropical cyclone in the Atlantic in 1965 and fifteenth since the retirement of tropical cyclone names officially began in <a href="/wiki/1954_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="1954 Atlantic hurricane season">1954</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TropicalCycloneNaming_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-TropicalCycloneNaming-126">[121]</a></sup> Consequently, the name was replaced with <a href="/wiki/1969_Atlantic_hurricane_season#Hurricane_Blanche" title="1969 Atlantic hurricane season">Blanche</a> for the 1969 season. Conversely, the name Blanche was used again in <a href="/wiki/1975_Atlantic_hurricane_season#Hurricane_Blanche" title="1975 Atlantic hurricane season">1975</a> before the <a href="/wiki/National_Hurricane_Center" title="National Hurricane Center">National Hurricane Center</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR1975_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-MWR1975-127">[122]</a></sup> still in its infancy at the time, handed control of tropical cyclone naming in its area of responsibility to the <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a> in 1977, after which the name Blanche was no longer used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCNATL_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-TCNATL-8">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hurricane_Betsy_in_popular_culture">Hurricane Betsy in popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Betsy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hurricane Betsy in popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>In April 1969,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LightninHopkinsAllMusic_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-LightninHopkinsAllMusic-128">[123]</a></sup> Texan <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a> artist <a href="/wiki/Lightnin%27_Hopkins" title="Lightnin' Hopkins">Sam John "Lightnin'" Hopkins</a> wrote the song <i>Hurricane Betsy</i> as the fourth track of the album <i>The Texas Bluesman</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LightninHopkinsAllMusic_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-LightninHopkinsAllMusic-128">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AppleLightnin_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-AppleLightnin-129">[124]</a></sup></li>
<li>The song <i>Georgia... Bush</i> was recorded by artists <a href="/wiki/DJ_Drama" title="DJ Drama">DJ Drama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lil_Wayne" title="Lil Wayne">Lil Wayne</a> and released along with the <a href="/wiki/Mixtape" title="Mixtape">mixtape</a> <a href="/wiki/Dedication_2" title="Dedication 2">Dedication 2</a> on September 4, 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dedication2_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dedication2-130">[125]</a></sup> Produced by Vudu Spellz, the song described a first-person account of <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a> and emasculated U.S. president <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>, while also indicting institutional racism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LilWayneCM_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-LilWayneCM-131">[126]</a></sup> Part of the song's lyrics – "Same shit happened back in Hurricane Betsy/ 1965" – makes direct reference to Hurricane Betsy, implying that the government intentionally destroyed levees such that the Lower Ninth Ward would be flooding and thus protecting more expensive lakefront property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LWLyrics_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-LWLyrics-132">[127]</a></sup></li>
<li>Written by Rachelle Burk and illustrated by Rex Schneider, the <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_literature" title="Children's literature">children's novel</a> <i>Tree House in a Storm</i> is a fictional telling of two child siblings whose tree house, having long served as a safe haven, is destroyed by Hurricane Betsy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmazonTreeHouseStorm_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmazonTreeHouseStorm-133">[128]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Betsy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">All monetary figures are in 1965 <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">United States dollars</a> unless otherwise noted.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Until the inception of the present-day <a href="/wiki/National_Hurricane_Center" title="National Hurricane Center">National Hurricane Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/1966_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="1966 Atlantic hurricane season">1966</a>, the United States Weather Bureau and later Miami Hurricane Warning Office handled tropical cyclone operations in the northern Atlantic basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WFOMiami_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-WFOMiami-6">[5]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Betsy saw the first use of the now-familiar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WC-130_Hercules" title="WC-130 Hercules">WC-130 Hercules</a> in the Hurricane Hunter role, with a "fix" mission into the eye of the storm on 27 August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuller_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-fuller-11">[9]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A major hurricane is a storm that ranks as Category 3 or higher on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_hurricane_wind_scale" title="Saffir–Simpson hurricane wind scale">Saffir–Simpson hurricane wind scale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BASIC_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-BASIC-17">[14]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The former Cuban province of Las Villas, initially known as Santa Clara until 1940,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ProvincesOfCuba_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-ProvincesOfCuba-48">[44]</a></sup> was split into the present-day provinces of <a href="/wiki/Villa_Clara_Province" title="Villa Clara Province">Villa Clara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cienfuegos_Province" title="Cienfuegos Province">Cienfuegos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sancti_Sp%C3%ADritus_Province" title="Sancti Spíritus Province">Sancti Spíritus</a> in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CubaGenWeb_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-CubaGenWeb-49">[45]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
</div>
